lang="en">Plasma (physics)</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Plasma_(disambiguation)" title="Plasma (disambiguation)">Plasma</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Plasma</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><span class="nowrap"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lightning3.jpg"><img alt="Lightning3.jpg" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21,_2011,_to_its_final_landing.jpg"><img alt="Space Shuttle Atlantis in the sky on July 21, 2011, to its final landing.jpg" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg/185px-Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg/278px-Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg/370px-Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg 2x" width="185"/></a></span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Top: <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">Lightning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neon_sign" title="Neon sign">neon lights</a> are commonplace generators of plasma. Bottom left: A <a href="/wiki/Plasma_globe" title="Plasma globe">plasma globe</a>, illustrating some of the more complex plasma phenomena, including <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)#Filamentation" title="Plasma (physics)">filamentation</a>. Bottom right: A plasma trail from the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis"><i>Atlantis</i></a> during re-entry into <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>, as seen from the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Plasma</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i>&#960;&#955;&#940;&#963;&#956;&#945;<i>&#8203;</i>, meaning \'moldable substance\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-&#960;&#955;&#940;&#963;&#956;&#945;_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-&#960;&#955;&#940;&#963;&#956;&#945;-1">[1]</a></sup>) is one of <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter#The_four_fundamental_states" title="State of matter">the four fundamental states of matter</a>, and was first described by chemist <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">Irving Langmuir</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpp1995_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Itpp1995-2">[2]</a></sup> in the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpd2012a_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Itpd2012a-3">[3]</a></sup> Plasma can be artificially generated by heating or subjecting a neutral gas to a strong <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a> to the point where an ionized gaseous substance becomes increasingly <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">electrically conductive</a>, and long-range electromagnetic fields dominate the behaviour of the matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpd2012b_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Itpd2012b-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Plasma and ionized gases have properties and display behaviours unlike those of the other states, and the <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">transition</a> between them is mostly a matter of nomenclature<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpp1995_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Itpp1995-2">[2]</a></sup> and subject to interpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpd2012_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Itpd2012-5">[5]</a></sup> Based on the surrounding environmental <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, partially <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionized</a> or fully ionized forms of plasma may be produced. <a href="/wiki/Neon_sign" title="Neon sign">Neon signs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> are examples of partially ionized plasma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> is a plasma and the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> contains plasma in the Earth\'s surrounding <a href="/wiki/Space_environment" title="Space environment">space environment</a>. The interior of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> is an example of fully ionized plasma,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1995_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips1995-7">[7]</a></sup> along with the <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">solar corona</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschwanden2004_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aschwanden2004-8">[8]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piel2010_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piel2010-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Positive <a href="/wiki/Charge_(physics)" title="Charge (physics)">charges</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">ions</a> are achieved by stripping away <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrons" title="Electrons">electrons</a> orbiting the atomic nuclei, where the total number of electrons removed is related to either increasing temperature or the local density of other ionized matter. This also can be accompanied by the dissociation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_bond" title="Molecular bond">molecular bonds</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturrock_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sturrock-10">[10]</a></sup> though this process is distinctly different from <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemical processes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Metal_ions_in_aqueous_solution" title="Metal ions in aqueous solution">ion interactions in liquids</a> or the behaviour of shared ions in <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>. The response of plasma to electromagnetic fields is used in many modern technological devices, such as <a href="/wiki/Plasma_display" title="Plasma display">plasma televisions</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plasma_etching" title="Plasma etching">plasma etching</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itptma2013_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Itptma2013-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Plasma may be the most abundant form of <a href="/wiki/Baryon#Baryonic_matter" title="Baryon">ordinary matter</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itptma2013a_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Itptma2013a-12">[12]</a></sup> although this hypothesis is currently tentative based on the existence and unknown properties of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>. Plasma is mostly associated with <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, extending to the rarefied <a href="/wiki/Intracluster_medium" title="Intracluster medium">intracluster medium</a> and possibly the <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic_space" title="Outer space">intergalactic regions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoPA2015_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-BoPA2015-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<p>The word <i>plasma</i> comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i>&#960;&#955;&#940;&#963;&#956;&#945;<i>&#8203;</i>, meaning \'moldable substance\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-&#960;&#955;&#940;&#963;&#956;&#945;_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-&#960;&#955;&#940;&#963;&#956;&#945;-1">[1]</a></sup> or \'jelly\',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Itpp1995_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Itpp1995-2">[2]</a></sup> and describes the behaviour of the ionized atomic nuclei and the electrons within the surrounding region of the plasma. Very simply, each of these nuclei are suspended in a movable sea of electrons. Plasma was first identified in a <a href="/wiki/Crookes_tube" title="Crookes tube">Crookes tube</a>, and so described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_William_Crookes" title="Sir William Crookes">Sir William Crookes</a> in 1879 (he called it "radiant matter").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The nature of this "<a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray" title="Cathode ray">cathode ray</a>" matter was subsequently identified by British physicist <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">Sir J.J. Thomson</a> in 1897.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term "plasma" was coined by <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">Irving Langmuir</a> in 1928.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langmuir1928_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-langmuir1928-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lewi_Tonks" title="Lewi Tonks">Lewi Tonks</a> and Harold Mott-Smith, both of whom worked with Irving Langmuir in the 1920s, recall that Langmuir first used the word "plasma" in analogy with blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Mott-Smith recalls, in particular, that the transport of electrons from thermionic filaments reminded Langmuir of "the way blood plasma carries red and white corpuscles and germs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Langmuir described the plasma he observed as follows:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>"<i>Except near the electrodes, where there are </i>sheaths<i> containing very few electrons, the ionized gas contains ions and electrons in about equal numbers so that the resultant space charge is very small. We shall use the name </i>plasma<i> to describe this region containing balanced charges of ions and electrons.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langmuir1928_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-langmuir1928-16">[16]</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#ddf; text-align:center;">Scientists</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div class="hlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Bernoulli" title="Daniel Bernoulli">Bernoulli</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Boyle</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Augustin-Louis_Cauchy" title="Augustin-Louis Cauchy">Cauchy</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Jacques_Charles" Gay-Lussac">Gay-Lussac</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Hooke</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Pascal</a></li>\n<li><a mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Continuum_mechanics" title="Template:Continuum mechanics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Continuum_mechanics" title="Template talk:Continuum mechanics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Continuum_mechanics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_parameters">Properties and parameters</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plasma_fountain.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Plasma_fountain.gif/260px-Plasma_fountain.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Plasma_fountain.gif/390px-Plasma_fountain.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/Plasma_fountain.gif 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plasma_fountain.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s <a href="/wiki/Artistic_rendering" title="Artistic rendering">rendition</a> of the Earth\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_fountain" title="Plasma fountain">plasma fountain</a>, showing oxygen, helium, and hydrogen ions that gush into space from regions near the Earth\'s poles. The faint yellow area shown above the north pole represents gas lost from Earth into space; the green area is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_borealis" title="Aurora borealis">aurora borealis</a>, where plasma energy pours back into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h3>\n<p>Plasma is a <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">state of matter</a> in which an ionized gaseous substance becomes highly <a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">electrically conductive</a> to the point that long-range <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electric and magnetic fields</a> dominate the behaviour of the matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen,_Francis_F._2016_2&#8211;3_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chen,_Francis_F._2016_2&#8211;3-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freidberg,_Jeffrey_P._2008_121_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freidberg,_Jeffrey_P._2008_121-22">[22]</a></sup> The plasma state can be contrasted with the <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter#The_four_fundamental_states" title="State of matter">other states</a>: <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a>.\n</p><p>Plasma is an electrically neutral medium of unbound positive and negative particles (i.e. the overall charge of a plasma is roughly zero). Although these particles are unbound, they are not "free" in the sense of not experiencing forces. Moving charged particles generate an electric current within a magnetic field, and any movement of a charged plasma particle affects and is affected by the fields created by the other charges. In turn this governs collective behaviour with many degrees of variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturrock_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sturrock-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Three factors define a plasma:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hazeltine_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hazeltine-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li><b>The plasma approximation</b>: The plasma approximation applies when the plasma parameter, &#923;,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> representing the number of charge carriers within a sphere (called the Debye sphere whose radius is the Debye screening length) surrounding a given charged particle, is sufficiently high as to shield the electrostatic influence of the particle outside of the sphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen,_Francis_F._2016_2&#8211;3_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chen,_Francis_F._2016_2&#8211;3-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freidberg,_Jeffrey_P._2008_121_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freidberg,_Jeffrey_P._2008_121-22">[22]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Bulk interactions</b>: The Debye screening length (defined above) is short compared to the physical size of the plasma. This criterion means that interactions in the bulk of the plasma are more important than those at its edges, where boundary effects may take place. When this criterion is satisfied, the plasma is quasineutral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Plasma frequency</b>: The electron plasma frequency (measuring <a href="/wiki/Plasma_oscillation" title="Plasma oscillation">plasma oscillations</a> of the electrons) is large compared to the electron-neutral collision frequency (measuring frequency of collisions between electrons and neutral particles). When this condition is valid, electrostatic interactions dominate over the processes of ordinary gas kinetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></li></ol>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature">Temperature</span></h3>\n<p>Plasma temperature is commonly measured in <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">electronvolts</a> and is, informally, a measure of the thermal kinetic energy per particle. High temperatures are usually needed to sustain ionisation, which is a defining feature of a plasma. The degree of plasma ionisation is determined by the <a href="/wiki/Electron_temperature" title="Electron temperature">electron temperature</a> relative to the <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energy</a> (and more weakly by the density), in a relationship called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saha_equation" title="Saha equation">Saha equation</a>. At low temperatures, ions and electrons tend to recombine into bound states&#8212;atoms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson-29">[29]</a></sup>&#8212;and the plasma will eventually become a gas.\n</p><p>In most cases the electrons are close enough to <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a> that their temperature is relatively well-defined; this is true even when there is a significant deviation from a <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell&#8211;Boltzmann distribution">Maxwellian</a> energy <a href="/wiki/Distribution_function_(physics)" title="Distribution function (physics)">distribution function</a>, for example, due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_radiation" title="UV radiation">UV radiation</a>, energetic particles, or strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_fields" title="Electric fields">electric fields</a>. Because of the large difference in mass, the electrons come to thermodynamic equilibrium amongst themselves much faster than they come into equilibrium with the ions or neutral atoms. For this reason, the ion temperature may be very different from (usually lower than) the electron temperature. This is especially common in weakly ionized technological plasmas, where the ions are often near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambient_temperature" title="Ambient temperature">ambient temperature</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Fully_vs._partially_.28weakly.29_ionized_gases"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fully_vs._partially_(weakly)_ionized_gases">Fully vs. partially (weakly) ionized gases</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_ionization" title="Degree of ionization">Degree of ionization</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_resistivity" title="Spitzer resistivity">Spitzer resistivity</a></div>\n<p>For plasma to exist, <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionisation</a> is necessary. The term "plasma density" by itself usually refers to the "electron density", that is, the number of free electrons per unit volume. The <a href="/wiki/Degree_of_ionization" title="Degree of ionization">degree of ionisation</a> of a plasma is the proportion of atoms that have lost or gained electrons, and is controlled by the electron and ion temperatures and electron-ion vs electron-neutral collision frequencies. The degree of ionisation, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#945;<!-- &#945; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\alpha }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\alpha " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b79333175c8b3f0840bfb4ec41b8072c83ea88d3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.488ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>, is defined as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\alpha ={\\frac {n_{i}}{n_{i}+n_{n}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#945;<!-- &#945; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\alpha ={\\frac {n_{i}}{n_{i}+n_{n}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\alpha = \\frac{n_i}{n_i + n_n}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3108711318f84fdb95253aaccfc613798e0831fd" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:13.07ex; height:5.009ex;"/></span>, where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n_{i}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n_{i}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/57f87f905ba5a4d8c691ccaecd65fc47bd007ba4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.194ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the number density of ions and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n_{n}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n_{n}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n_n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe4524072cf7277c48ce6b7447393aed66198f9d" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.613ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the number density of neutral atoms. The <i>electron density</i> is related to this by the average charge state<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (August 2018)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\langle Z\\rangle }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10216;<!-- &#10216; --></mo>\n<mi>Z</mi>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\langle Z\\rangle }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\langle Z\\rangle" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fd61c8ebfead0b5e67185bce74f42061e3f8d1a5" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:3.49ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> of the ions through <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n_{e}=\\langle Z\\rangle n_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10216;<!-- &#10216; --></mo>\n<mi>Z</mi>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n_{e}=\\langle Z\\rangle n_{i}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n_e = \\langle Z\\rangle n_i" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b1193ff1fbe003293adab62d868adbe4d2555174" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.176ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n_{e}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n_{e}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n_{e}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5e21303faca6e2167f4ecbf2a75aed982e817035" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.393ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the number density of electrons.\n</p><p>In a plasma, the electron-ion collision frequency <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/55375b2556adb6b79be144fcb88c7da728e4d65a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.714ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is much greater than the electron-neutral collision frequency <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{en}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu _{en}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{en}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dc4372e734a4f0773f0146ef41bee08d2c8d82b9" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.133ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>. Therefore, with a weak degree of ionization <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#945;<!-- &#945; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\alpha }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\alpha " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b79333175c8b3f0840bfb4ec41b8072c83ea88d3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.488ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>, the electron-ion collision frequency can equal the electron-neutral collision frequency: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}=\\nu _{en}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}=\\nu _{en}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}=\\nu _{en}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8945bae1befbe3b5aebaab8987aeb6462ca961c7" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.946ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the limit separating a plasma from being partially or fully ionized.\n</p>\n<ul><li>The term <i>fully ionized gas</i> introduced by <a href="/wiki/Lyman_Spitzer" title="Lyman Spitzer">Lyman Spitzer</a> does not mean the degree of ionization is unity, but only that the plasma is in a <i>Coulomb-collision dominated regime</i>, i.e. when <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}&gt;\\nu _{en}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&gt;</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}&gt;\\nu _{en}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}&gt;\\nu _{en}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/31e9faa0b1cda6b19b48722980ae93688d1100b8" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.946ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, which can correspond to a degree of ionization as low as 0.01%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman_1980_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman_1980-30">[30]</a></sup></li>\n<li>A <i>partially</i> or <i>weakly ionized gas</i> means the plasma is not dominated by <a href="/wiki/Coulomb_collision" title="Coulomb collision">Coulomb collisions</a>, i.e. when <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}&lt;\\nu _{en}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&lt;</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}&lt;\\nu _{en}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\nu _{ei}&lt;\\nu _{en}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3024188848c7a463c4cc3a4281db9fa1c3d08931" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.946ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>.</li></ul>\n<p>Most of "technological" (engineered) plasmas are weakly ionized gases.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Thermal_vs._nonthermal_.28cold.29_plasmas"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_vs._nonthermal_(cold)_plasmas">Thermal vs. nonthermal (cold) plasmas</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nonthermal_plasma" title="Nonthermal plasma">Nonthermal plasma</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anisothermal_plasma" title="Anisothermal plasma">Anisothermal plasma</a></div>\n<p>Based on the relative temperatures of the electrons, ions and neutrals, plasmas are classified as "thermal" or "non-thermal" (also referred to as "cold plasmas").\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Thermal plasmas</i> have electrons and the heavy particles at the same temperature, i.e. they are in thermal equilibrium with each other.</li>\n<li><i>Nonthermal plasmas</i> on the other hand are non-equilibrium ionized gases, with two temperatures: ions and neutrals stay at a low temperature (sometimes <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>), whereas electrons are much hotter. (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle T_{e}\\gg T_{n}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8811;<!-- &#8811; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle T_{e}\\gg T_{n}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="T_e \\gg T_n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2843eb098a1abed6f66b9af0a0ca1023b90c8ebb" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.546ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> A kind of common nonthermal plasma is the <a href="/wiki/Mercury-vapor_lamp" title="Mercury-vapor lamp">mercury-vapor gas</a> within a <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamp</a>, where the "electrons gas" reaches a temperature of 10,000 kelvins while the rest of the gas stays barely above room temperature, so the <a href="/wiki/Electric_light" title="Electric light">bulb</a> can even be touched with hands while operating.</li></ul>\n<p>A particular and unusual case of "inverse" nonthermal plasma is the very high temperature plasma produced by the <a href="/wiki/Z_Pulsed_Power_Facility" title="Z Pulsed Power Facility">Z machine</a>, where ions are much hotter than electrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haines_a_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-haines_a-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haines_b_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-haines_b-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma_potential">Plasma potential</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg/350px-Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg/525px-Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg/700px-Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bliksem_in_Assen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">Lightning</a> as an example of plasma present at Earth\'s surface: Typically, lightning discharges 30 kiloamperes at up to 100 megavolts, and emits radio waves, light, X- and even gamma rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Plasma temperatures can approach 30000 K and electron densities may exceed 10<sup>24</sup> m<sup>&#8722;3</sup>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Since plasmas are very good <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductors</a>, electric potentials play an important role.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The average potential in the space between charged particles, independent of how it can be measured, is called the "plasma potential", or the "space potential". If an electrode is inserted into a plasma, its potential will generally lie considerably below the plasma potential due to what is termed a <a href="/wiki/Debye_sheath" title="Debye sheath">Debye sheath</a>. The good electrical conductivity of plasmas makes their electric fields very small. This results in the important concept of "quasineutrality", which says the density of negative charges is approximately equal to the density of positive charges over large volumes of the plasma (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n_{e}=\\langle Z\\rangle n_{i}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10216;<!-- &#10216; --></mo>\n<mi>Z</mi>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>i</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n_{e}=\\langle Z\\rangle n_{i}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n_e = \\langle Z\\rangle n_i" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b1193ff1fbe003293adab62d868adbe4d2555174" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.176ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>), but on the scale of the <a href="/wiki/Debye_length" title="Debye length">Debye length</a> there can be charge imbalance. In the special case that <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_layer_(plasma)" title="Double layer (plasma)">double layers</a></i> are formed, the charge separation can extend some tens of Debye lengths.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The magnitude of the potentials and electric fields must be determined by means other than simply finding the net <a href="/wiki/Charge_density" title="Charge density">charge density</a>. A common example is to assume that the electrons satisfy the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_relation" title="Boltzmann relation">Boltzmann relation</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n_{e}\\propto e^{e\\Phi /k_{B}T_{e}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8733;<!-- &#8733; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#934;<!-- &#934; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n_{e}\\propto e^{e\\Phi /k_{B}T_{e}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n_{e}\\propto e^{e\\Phi /k_{B}T_{e}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f7469b31fe8ad01e161bccadd28bccea4e189fb6" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:14.008ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Differentiating this relation provides a means to calculate the electric field from the density:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\vec {E}}=(k_{B}T_{e}/e)(\\nabla n_{e}/n_{e}).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8594;<!-- &#8594; --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>k</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8711;<!-- &#8711; --></mi>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\vec {E}}=(k_{B}T_{e}/e)(\\nabla n_{e}/n_{e}).}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\vec{E} = (k_BT_e/e)(\\nabla n_e/n_e)." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/631406a82915c1f6add73c3592d1f7cbf2dc459c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:24.317ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>It is possible to produce a plasma that is not quasineutral. An electron beam, for example, has only negative charges. The density of a non-neutral plasma must generally be very low, or it must be very small, otherwise, it will be dissipated by the repulsive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrostatic_force" title="Electrostatic force">electrostatic force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrophysical" title="Astrophysical">astrophysical</a> plasmas, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_field_screening" title="Electric field screening">Debye screening</a> prevents <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric fields</a> from directly affecting the plasma over large distances, i.e., greater than the <a href="/wiki/Debye_length" title="Debye length">Debye length</a>. However, the existence of charged particles causes the plasma to generate, and be affected by, <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>. This can and does cause extremely complex behaviour, such as the generation of plasma double layers, an object that separates charge over a few tens of <a href="/wiki/Debye_length" title="Debye length">Debye lengths</a>. The dynamics of plasmas interacting with external and self-generated <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a> are studied in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" title="Academic discipline">academic discipline</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">magnetohydrodynamics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetization">Magnetization</span></h3>\n<p>Plasma with a magnetic field strong enough to influence the motion of the charged particles is said to be magnetized. A common quantitative criterion is that a particle on average completes at least one gyration around the magnetic field before making a collision, i.e., <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\omega _{\\mathrm {ce} }/v_{\\mathrm {coll} }&gt;1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&gt;</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\omega _{\\mathrm {ce} }/v_{\\mathrm {coll} }&gt;1}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\omega_{\\mathrm{ce}} / v_{\\mathrm{coll}} &gt; 1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/21f1862bc2cdc02a507ae2e79a1d0f3cdeabcca6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:12.388ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\omega _{\\mathrm {ce} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#969;<!-- &#969; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\omega _{\\mathrm {ce} }}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\omega_{\\mathrm{ce}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a77c94d2b62f2fbdb8c001c497f3987cf5b46d5c" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.138ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the "electron gyrofrequency" and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v_{\\mathrm {coll} }}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v_{\\mathrm {coll} }}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v_{\\mathrm{coll}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ce453e7cf357667cbd5dec2fe68db30b003d6154" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.827ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is the "electron collision rate". It is often the case that the electrons are magnetized while the ions are not. Magnetized plasmas are <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anisotropic" title="Anisotropic">anisotropic</a></i>, meaning that their properties in the direction parallel to the magnetic field are different from those perpendicular to it. While electric fields in plasmas are usually small due to the high conductivity, the electric field associated with a plasma moving in a magnetic field is given by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} =-v\\times \\mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>&#215;<!-- &#215; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} =-v\\times \\mathbf {B} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf{E} = -v\\times\\mathbf{B}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/985221cedcc22c662aa027dda81134b51b67c10e" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:12.533ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> (where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf {E} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0d7f22b39d51f780fc02859059c1757c606b9de2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.757ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the electric field, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {v} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">v</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {v} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf {v} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/35c1866e359fbfd2e0f606c725ba5cc37a5195d6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.411ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the velocity, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {B} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf {B} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cafb0ef39b0f5ffa23c170aa7f7b4e718327c4d1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.901ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the magnetic field), and is not affected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debye_shielding" title="Debye shielding">Debye shielding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_of_plasma_and_gas_phases">Comparison of plasma and gas phases</span></h3>\n<p>Plasma is often called the <i>fourth state of matter</i> after solid, liquids and gases, despite plasma typically being an ionized gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> It is distinct from these and other lower-energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_matter" title="States of matter">states of matter</a>. Although it is closely related to the gas phase in that it also has no definite form or volume, it differs in a number of ways, including the following:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Property</th>\n<th>Gas</th>\n<th>Plasma\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_and_conductivity" title="Electrical resistivity and conductivity">Electrical conductivity</a>\n</th>\n<td><b>Very low</b>: Air is an excellent insulator until it breaks down into plasma at electric field strengths above 30 kilovolts per centimeter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td><b>Usually very high</b>: For many purposes, the conductivity of a plasma may be treated as infinite.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Independently acting species\n</th>\n<td><b>One</b>: All gas particles behave in a similar way, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Collision" title="Collision">collisions</a> with one another.\n</td>\n<td><b>Two or three</b>: <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">Electrons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> can be distinguished by the sign and value of their <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">charge</a> so that they behave independently in many circumstances, with different bulk velocities and temperatures, allowing phenomena such as new types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waves_in_plasma" title="Waves in plasma">waves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Instability" title="Instability">instabilities</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Velocity distribution\n</th>\n<td><b><a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell&#8211;Boltzmann distribution">Maxwellian</a></b>: Collisions usually lead to a Maxwellian velocity distribution of all gas particles, with very few relatively fast particles.\n</td>\n<td><b>Often non-Maxwellian</b>: Collisional interactions are often weak in hot plasmas and external forcing can drive the plasma far from local equilibrium and lead to a significant population of unusually fast particles.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Interactions\n</th>\n<td><b>Binary</b>: Two-particle collisions are the rule, three-body collisions extremely rare.\n</td>\n<td><b>Collective</b>: Waves, or organized motion of plasma, are very important because the particles can interact at long ranges through the electric and magnetic forces.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasmas_in_space_science_and_astronomy">Plasmas in space science and astronomy</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_plasma" title="Astrophysical plasma">Astrophysical plasma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">Interstellar medium</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intergalactic_space" title="Intergalactic space">Intergalactic space</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">Magnetohydrodynamics</a></div>\n<p>Plasmas are by far the most common <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phase of ordinary matter</a> in the universe, both by mass and by volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Above the Earth\'s surface, the ionosphere is a plasma,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and the magnetosphere contains plasma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Within our Solar System, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interplanetary_space" title="Interplanetary space">interplanetary space</a> is filled with the plasma expelled via the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, extending from the Sun\'s surface out to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)#Heliopause" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">heliopause</a>. Furthermore, all the distant <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, and much of <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar space</a> or <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic_space" title="Outer space">intergalactic space</a> is also likely filled with plasma, albeit at very low densities. <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_plasma" title="Astrophysical plasma">Astrophysical plasmas</a> are also observed in <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disk" title="Accretion disk">Accretion disks</a> around stars or compact objects like <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_holes" title="Black holes">black holes</a> in close <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Plasma is associated with ejection of material in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrophysical_jets" title="Astrophysical jets">astrophysical jets</a>, which have been observed with accreting black holes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> or in active <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a> like <a href="/wiki/Messier_87#Jet" title="Messier 87">M87\'s jet</a> that possibly extends out to 5,000 light-years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_plasmas">Common plasmas</span></h2>\n<p>Plasmas can appear in nature in various forms and locations, which can be usefully broadly summarised in the following Table:\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Common forms of plasma\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="width: 40%;">Artificially produced\n</th>\n<th style="width: 30%;">Terrestrial plasmas\n</th>\n<th style="width: 30%;">Space and astrophysical plasmas\n</th></tr>\n<tr style="vertical-align: top;">\n<td>\n<ul><li>Those found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_displays" title="Plasma displays">plasma displays</a>, including TV screens.</li>\n<li>Inside <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a> (low energy lighting), <a href="/wiki/Neon_sign" title="Neon sign">neon signs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Rocket exhaust and <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">ion thrusters</a></li>\n<li>The area in front of a <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Heat_shield" title="Heat shield">heat shield</a> during <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">re-entry</a> into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">atmosphere</a></li>\n<li>Inside a corona discharge <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> generator</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_energy" title="Fusion energy">Fusion energy</a> research</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">electric arc</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Arc_lamp" title="Arc lamp">arc lamp</a>, an arc <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welder</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plasma_torch" title="Plasma torch">plasma torch</a></li>\n<li>Plasma ball (sometimes called a plasma sphere or <a href="/wiki/Plasma_globe" title="Plasma globe">plasma globe</a>)</li>\n<li>Arcs produced by <a href="/wiki/Tesla_coil" title="Tesla coil">Tesla coils</a> (resonant air core transformer or disruptor coil that produces arcs similar to lightning, but with <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">alternating current</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">static electricity</a>)</li>\n<li>Plasmas used in <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor_device_fabrication" title="Semiconductor device fabrication">semiconductor device fabrication</a> including <a href="/wiki/Reactive-ion_etching" title="Reactive-ion etching">reactive-ion etching</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">sputtering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plasma_cleaning" title="Plasma cleaning">surface cleaning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plasma-enhanced_chemical_vapor_deposition" title="Plasma-enhanced chemical vapor deposition">plasma-enhanced chemical vapor deposition</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">Laser</a>-produced plasmas (LPP), found when high power lasers interact with materials.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Inductively_coupled_plasma" title="Inductively coupled plasma">Inductively coupled plasmas</a> (ICP), formed typically in <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> gas for optical emission <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a></li>\n<li>Magnetically induced plasmas (MIP), typically produced using microwaves as a resonant coupling method</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">Static electric sparks</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Capacitively_coupled_plasma" title="Capacitively coupled plasma">Capacitively coupled plasmas</a> (CCP)</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_Barrier_Discharge" title="Dielectric Barrier Discharge">Dielectric Barrier Discharges</a> (DBD)</li></ul>\n</td>\n<td>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">Lightning</a></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> contains plasma in the Earth\'s surrounding space environment</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Plasmasphere" title="Plasmasphere">plasmasphere</a></li>\n<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">polar aurorae</a></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Polar_wind" title="Polar wind">polar wind</a>, a plasma fountain</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Upper-atmospheric_lightning" title="Upper-atmospheric lightning">Upper-atmospheric lightning</a> (e.g. Blue jets, Blue starters, Gigantic jets, ELVES)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sprite_(lightning)" title="Sprite (lightning)">Sprites</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/St._Elmo%27s_fire" title="St. Elmo\'s fire">St. Elmo\'s fire</a></li></ul>\n</td>\n<td>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">Stars</a><br/>(plasmas heated by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>)</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary medium</a><br/>(space between planets)</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a><br/>(space between star systems)</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic_space" title="Outer space">Intergalactic medium</a><br/>(space between galaxies)</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>-<a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> <a href="/wiki/Flux_tube" title="Flux tube">flux tube</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" title="Accretion disc">Accretion discs</a></li>\n<li>Interstellar <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a></li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_plasma_phenomena">Complex plasma phenomena</span></h2>\n<p>Although the underlying equations governing plasmas are relatively simple, plasma behaviour is extraordinarily varied and subtle: the emergence of unexpected behaviour from a simple model is a typical feature of a <a href="/wiki/Complex_system" title="Complex system">complex system</a>. Such systems lie in some sense on the boundary between ordered and disordered behaviour and cannot typically be described either by simple, smooth, mathematical functions, or by pure randomness. The spontaneous formation of interesting spatial features on a wide range of length scales is one manifestation of plasma complexity. The features are interesting, for example, because they are very sharp, spatially intermittent (the distance between features is much larger than the features themselves), or have a <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a> form. Many of these features were first studied in the laboratory, and have subsequently been recognized throughout the universe. Examples of complexity and complex structures in plasmas include:\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Filamentation">Filamentation</span></h3>\n<p>Striations or string-like structures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> also known as <a href="/wiki/Birkeland_current" title="Birkeland current">Birkeland currents</a>, are seen in many plasmas, like the <a href="/wiki/Plasma_globe" title="Plasma globe">plasma ball</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurora</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">electric arcs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_flares" title="Solar flares">solar flares</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> They are sometimes associated with larger current densities, and the interaction with the magnetic field can form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_rope" title="Magnetic rope">magnetic rope</a> structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> High power microwave breakdown at atmospheric pressure also leads to the formation of filamentary structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mwbrkdwn_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-mwbrkdwn-55">[55]</a></sup> (See also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_pinch" title="Plasma pinch">Plasma pinch</a>)\n</p><p>Filamentation also refers to the self-focusing of a high power laser pulse. At high powers, the nonlinear part of the index of refraction becomes important and causes a higher index of refraction in the center of the laser beam, where the laser is brighter than at the edges, causing a feedback that focuses the laser even more. The tighter focused laser has a higher peak brightness (irradiance) that forms a plasma. The plasma has an index of refraction lower than one, and causes a defocusing of the laser beam. The interplay of the focusing index of refraction, and the defocusing plasma makes the formation of a long filament of plasma that can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometer_(unit)" title="Micrometer (unit)">micrometers</a> to kilometers in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> One interesting aspect of the filamentation generated plasma is the relatively low ion density due to defocusing effects of the ionized electrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> (See also <a href="/wiki/Filament_propagation" title="Filament propagation">Filament propagation</a>)\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-neutral_plasma">Non-neutral plasma</span></h3>\n<p>The strength and range of the electric force and the good conductivity of plasmas usually ensure that the densities of positive and negative charges in any sizeable region are equal ("quasineutrality"). A plasma with a significant excess of charge density, or, in the extreme case, is composed of a single species, is called a <a href="/wiki/Non-neutral_plasmas" title="Non-neutral plasmas">non-neutral plasma</a>. In such a plasma, electric fields play a dominant role. Examples are charged <a href="/wiki/Particle_beam" title="Particle beam">particle beams</a>, an electron cloud in a <a href="/wiki/Penning_trap" title="Penning trap">Penning trap</a> and positron plasmas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Dusty_plasma.2Fgrain_plasma"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dusty_plasma/grain_plasma">Dusty plasma/grain plasma</span></h3>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Dusty_plasma" title="Dusty plasma">dusty plasma</a> contains tiny charged particles of dust (typically found in space). The dust particles acquire high charges and interact with each other. A plasma that contains larger particles is called grain plasma. Under laboratory conditions, dusty plasmas are also called <i>complex plasmas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impermeable_plasma">Impermeable plasma</span></h3>\n<p>Impermeable plasma is a type of thermal plasma which acts like an impermeable solid with respect to gas or cold plasma and can be physically pushed. Interaction of cold gas and thermal plasma was briefly studied by a group led by <a href="/wiki/Hannes_Alfv%C3%A9n" title="Hannes Alfv&#233;n">Hannes Alfv&#233;n</a> in 1960s and 1970s for its possible applications in insulation of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a> plasma from the reactor walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> However, later it was found that the external <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_fields" title="Magnetic fields">magnetic fields</a> in this configuration could induce <a href="/wiki/Kink_instability" title="Kink instability">kink instabilities</a> in the plasma and subsequently lead to an unexpectedly high heat loss to the walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\nIn 2013, a group of materials scientists reported that they have successfully generated stable impermeable plasma with no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_confinement" title="Magnetic confinement">magnetic confinement</a> using only an ultrahigh-pressure blanket of cold gas. While spectroscopic data on the characteristics of plasma were claimed to be difficult to obtain due to the high pressure, the passive effect of plasma on <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthesis</a> of different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanostructures" title="Nanostructures">nanostructures</a> clearly suggested the effective confinement. They also showed that upon maintaining the impermeability for a few tens of seconds, screening of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">ions</a> at the plasma-gas interface could give rise to a strong secondary mode of heating (known as viscous heating) leading to different kinetics of reactions and formation of complex <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_descriptions">Mathematical descriptions</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_rope.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="262" decoding="async" height="363" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Magnetic_rope.svg/220px-Magnetic_rope.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Magnetic_rope.svg/330px-Magnetic_rope.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Magnetic_rope.svg/440px-Magnetic_rope.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Magnetic_rope.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The complex self-constricting magnetic field lines and current paths in a field-aligned <a href="/wiki/Birkeland_current" title="Birkeland current">Birkeland current</a> that can develop in a plasma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plasma_modeling" title="Plasma modeling">Plasma modeling</a></div>\n<p>To completely describe the state of a plasma, all of the\nparticle locations and velocities that describe the electromagnetic field in the plasma region would need to be written down.\nHowever, it is generally not practical or necessary to keep track of all the particles in a plasma.\nTherefore, plasma physicists commonly use less detailed descriptions, of which\nthere are two main types:\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluid_model">Fluid model</span></h3>\n<p>Fluid models describe plasmas in terms of smoothed quantities, like density and averaged velocity around each position (see <a href="/wiki/Plasma_parameters" title="Plasma parameters">Plasma parameters</a>). One simple fluid model, <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">magnetohydrodynamics</a>, treats the plasma as a single fluid governed by a combination of <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">Maxwell\'s equations</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Navier%E2%80%93Stokes_equations" title="Navier&#8211;Stokes equations">Navier&#8211;Stokes equations</a>. A more general description is the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Two-fluid_plasma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Two-fluid plasma (page does not exist)">two-fluid plasma</a> picture, where the ions and electrons are described separately. Fluid models are often accurate when collisionality is sufficiently high to keep the plasma velocity distribution close to a <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell&#8211;Boltzmann distribution">Maxwell&#8211;Boltzmann distribution</a>. Because fluid models usually describe the plasma in terms of a single flow at a certain temperature at each spatial location, they can neither capture velocity space structures like beams or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_layer_(plasma)" title="Double layer (plasma)">double layers</a>, nor resolve wave-particle effects.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinetic_model">Kinetic model</span></h3>\n<p>Kinetic models describe the particle velocity distribution function at each point in the plasma and therefore do not need to assume a <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell&#8211;Boltzmann distribution">Maxwell&#8211;Boltzmann distribution</a>. A kinetic description is often necessary for collisionless plasmas. There are two common approaches to kinetic description of a plasma. One is based on representing the smoothed distribution function on a grid in velocity and position. The other, known as the <a href="/wiki/Particle-in-cell" title="Particle-in-cell">particle-in-cell</a> (PIC) technique, includes kinetic information by following the trajectories of a large number of individual particles. Kinetic models are generally more computationally intensive than fluid models. The <a href="/wiki/Vlasov_equation" title="Vlasov equation">Vlasov equation</a> may be used to describe the dynamics of a system of charged particles interacting with an electromagnetic field.\nIn magnetized plasmas, a <a href="/wiki/Gyrokinetics" title="Gyrokinetics">gyrokinetic</a> approach can substantially reduce the computational expense of a fully kinetic simulation.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial_plasmas">Artificial plasmas</span></h2>\n<p>Most artificial plasmas are generated by the application of electric and/or magnetic fields through a gas. Plasma generated in a laboratory setting and for industrial use can be generally categorized by:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The type of power source used to generate the plasma&#8212;DC, AC (typically with <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">radio frequency</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RF" title="RF">RF</a>)) and microwave</li>\n<li>The pressure they operate at&#8212;vacuum pressure (&lt;
